B000SEIK2S|4.0|John Perry is a good guy, an every man, a born fighter and a natural leader. He makes friends easily, he commands respect wherever he goes, and his tactical nous on the battlefield is very strong. We get to share the surprises and wonders in the story with him, we share in his losses, and we most definitely share in his wins.<br /><br />That said, John seems to be just a little too perfect, just a little too quick with the answer to a deadly problem, and just a little too natural a leader. He always makes the right decisions, he always overcomes improbably odds, and he is always left standing at the end whilst people are dying around him. I liked him, but I just wish he was a little more fallible.<br /><br />The strength of any Scalzi novel, for me, is the dialogue, and Old Man's War is no different. Scalzi puts a lot of time and effort into crafting personalities, and the banter between personalities is quick, witty, and often time profound. Whether its talking about calorie-laden breakfasts or trying to be diplomatic with an aggressive alien species, the dialogue is never in the backseat position and I think that's a good thing. I know I laughed out loud on more than one occasion.<br /><br />Old Man's War is a lot of fun, a science fiction novel that doesn't require you to think too hard about the science. This is probably a weak point for those who prefer their science fiction to be very accurate and specific, but for those looking to get into the genre I think this would be a very good starter book .

B003P2WO5E|5.0|I've just finished reading this book for the second time, and I don't think I'm even close to unveiling all the mysteries and secrets Sanderson has hidden in there. If you have read the Mistborn series, you will know how things Sanderson briefly mentions in the first chapter of the first book are actually key parts of the mysteries unveiled in the final book. You would also be familiar with Kelsier and his favourite saying - \\"Every secret has a secret.\\"  The Way of Kings is no different, with seemingly random sentences scattered through the book meaning absolutely nothing unless you know the context to which they apply. I am still trying to process the amount of information I learned during my re-read of The Way of Kings. The knowledge you gain during your first read in the latter parts of the book, like knowing how long it takes to summon a shardblade, will actually reveal hidden secrets about different characters during your re-read of the early parts of the book. If you loved this book and you thought you knew exactly what was going on, I implore you to read the book again and see if you change your mind.<br /><br />The thing about this 1000 page door stopper is that its a daunting proposition, and honestly, I would not recommend it if you are new to fantasy or new to Brandon Sanderson. Read one of his shorter works first like The Emperor's Soul. Read the Mistborn trilogy first. Read Warbreaker first (it is available as a free download from Sanderson's website). If you are an epic fantasy enthusiast and you have not read this book, finish whatever book you are currently reading and make this your next book. It is a unique experience that you should not miss out on, but only if you have done some prior preparation.

B00BIVN82M|3.0|I became a Michael Buble fan rather late, just a year or so ago.  But I quickly bought most of his LPs and have enjoyed all of them.  Sometimes, I love the way he deconstructs a song, casting aside the usual arrangement and reinventing it as a different song.  To me the prime example of this is his version of the old standard \\"Cry Me a River\\" which becomes a staggering, defiant song of angst rather than the usual sappy weeper it usually seems to be.  But no great examples of Buble's style, such as \\"River\\", are found on this current LP.<br /><br />I tend to agree with the other reviewers on here who say that this LP seems to force Michael Buble out of his comfort zone.  Not a bad thing to try, certainly, and I can't say there are any really bad songs on this album.  However, Buble seems to be trying to sing more in an Uptown R&B mode rather than the saloon singer/crooner with an edge where he seems most natural.  I find some of the arrangements too \\"bright,\\" I would say, not a good background to his supple, slightly gritty baritone.<br /><br />Definitely, Buble is not an R&B singer.  I was looking forward to his rendering of two songs with Motown lineages, \\"Who's Loving You\\" and \\"To Be Loved,\\" the former originally performed by Smokey Robinson and the Miracles and the latter by Jackie Wilson.  But \\"Loving You\\" is too jazzy, not plaintive enough.  Michael Jackson on the very first Jackson 5 LP way back in '69 or '70 performed it much better.  \\"To Be Loved\\" as well just comes across as forced sounding and almost a pose, something I find in many of Harry Connick's recordings, and with a dull MOR background.  I found the arrangement of \\"Something Stupid\\" charming but the vocals a little too reminiscent of Frank and Nancy Sinatra (Nancy, like the non-singer on this recording, certainly didn't have her father's pipes) to offer anything new.  Even the Bee Gees' classic \\"To Love Sombeody\\" which actually has been recorded by many R&B and even jazz singers including the SweetInspirations and Nina Simone with great success seems oddly ill suited in a too upbeat arrangement with Buble almost shouting the lyrics on what should be a song which emphasizes regret and longing in an understated way.<br /><br />Overall, I think the choice of material was intriguing but the arrangements too \\"aggressive\\" and bright, and Buble's vocals too far out of sync with the endearing and yearning qualities which have really earned him his stripes over the years.<br /><br />Hopefully, the album will grow on me.  As I said, there's not really a bad song on it.  But overall, Buble sounds like he was forced into a vocal arena where he really doesn't belong, trying to compete with noisy and unmusical arrangements and therefore having to sing more like an R&B shouter than the song stylist he really is.

B006PZBR8S|5.0|When I first saw previews for this movie, my first thought was \\"Hey, they finally made a movie out of the Rock 'Em Sock 'Em Robots game.\\"  Silly, I know, but in this day and age where they can make a successful film franchise out of a toy line (the \\"Transformers\\" films) and a Disneyland ride (the \\"Pirates of the Caribbean\\" films), then why not a game?  It could work if they have the right people behind it, right?  (This was before the bomb that was \\"Battleship\\" came out, mind you...)<br /><br />Unlike the aforementioned \\"Battleship\\" movie, however, \\"Real Steel\\" is a wonderful movie.  It's not a heavy science-fiction film, or really even an action film despite featuring fighting robots.  It's a film with heart behind its metallic brawn, and even though the robots get plenty of screen time it's the human element that makes this film shine.<br /><br />Based loosely on a Richard Matheson short story, \\"Real Steel\\" follows Charlie, a washed-up former boxing champ turned fighting-robot manager who's deeply in debt and has just lost his last fighting robot in a particularly brutal match.  He finds himself a reluctant caretaker to his estranged son, Max, and ends up taking the boy with him as he travels the country, trying to scrape together funds to buy or build a new robot and pay his debts.  When Max comes across an outdated sparring bot abandoned in a junkyard and decides to restore it and fight it, Charlie is skeptical... but Atom, Max's bot, turns out to be far tougher than anyone realizes, and soon Atom becomes a sensation in the underground fighting league.  And it may be Atom that ends up drawing father and son together.<br /><br />The plot of this movie is fairly predictable -- you've probably seen it in a hundred sports movies before and are likely to see it again.  But that doesn't mean the plot doesn't work here.  The premise of a near-future where robots do the fighting in the boxing ring adds a fresh twist to the plot, and the characters bring a lot of heart to their roles.  Hugh Jackman proves to be a versatile, phenomenal actor, and brings a gruff pathos to his role as Charlie.  Max starts off as the typical whiny child, but develops into a likable and spunky kid as the movie progresses.  The \\"villains\\" of this film (the creator and manager of the champion fighting bot Zeus, and a borderline-sadistic promoter to whom Charlie owes money) are fairly one-note and flat, but that just makes it all the more satisfying when they get their just dues.  And Atom, despite being mute and personality-less throughout the film, somehow manages to win the viewers heart as well -- who doesn't like an underdog story, and want to see the little guy win?<br /><br />The visual effects in this film are phenomenal as well.  The film is portrayed as a \\"near-future\\" where many elements are similar, but the robots don't come across as a jarring addition.  At times it's easy to forget one is watching CGI effects and not actual machinery, and each of the fighting bots has its own unique look.  It makes me wish this movie had come with a decent toy line, because I know I would buy myself a miniature version of Atom or Noisy Boy if I could.  Oddly enough, the most jarring \\"special effects failure\\" in the film comes from the fight in the opening scene, where a fighting robot takes on a bull -- it's quite easy to tell when they stop using an actual animal and start using a CGI dummy.  But then, visual effects haven't come far enough to produce animals that don't look fake, I suppose...<br /><br />This film has been described as \\"Rocky with robots,\\" and I have to say it's an accurate description.  It has a LOT of heart, great acting, and a story that has been told many times but manages to not be stale or cheesy here.  Even if you're not a sci-fi fan, I'd give this movie a look -- it's a good heartwarming film, and the robots are just the cherry on top for me.<br /><br />Recommended for ages 10 and up, due to language and violence.o their roles. B0008ENHUI|4.0|Papillon is a great film. It's a brilliant, action packed, and memorable story of one man's indomitable will, survival, strength, determination, and unquenchable courage.<br /><br />The cast is excellent - with Steve McQueen stealing the scenes and carrying the entire film on his back with his electrifying performance as prisoner Henri Charriere, or Papillon - the butterfly. I do not believe the movie would be the same without the King of Cool. He turns in a performance of surprising depth, weight, nuance, and incredible acting. Dustin Hoffman is good too as Papillon's companion Dega, but there is nothing showy in his performance, just solid, excellent acting.<br /><br />The story could use a bit of work. It is based on Charriere's memoir of the same name, which, according to researchers, is about 50% fiction. The movie itself veers from the book, expanding a questionable side turn with an Indian tribe, and a lengthily tattoo sequence that adds nothing to the plot. I felt the story petered out towards the last half of the film. Interesting fact, the real Charriere was in his early 40's when he escaped Devil's Island, not old and white-haired and feeble like the movie implies.<br /><br />But I have no problems with the incredible sets - filmed on location in Puerto Rico and French Guyana, the incredible prison scenes that accurately display life in a 1940's work camp. The rainforests, the beaches, the sunny locales, the mangroves, the deserts...all are displayed excellently onscreen on Papillon. The tone of the film is good too, with thrills, emotion, double crosses, suspense, horror, romance, and even some humour thrown into the mix.<br /><br />I believe the best part of the film takes place when Charriere is in solitary confinement after a failed escape attempt. The entire 30 minute scene takes place inside his solitary cell. There are no frills, just 3 actors, one set, one scene, and incredible acting from Steve McQueen. And even more incredible, most of the scenes are silent, without dialogue. The scene shows his indomitable will, his determination, his humanity, and most of all, a brilliant portrayal of hope.<br /><br />There is an incredible scene in the solitary confinement episode - a mad scene by Papillon - that is such an stunning display of acting that I found myself lost in the character's plight, awed by McQueen's skills. The King of Cool proved he was not only slick and cool as hell, but also an incredible actor. Another brilliant scene is where the sadistic guards curtain Papillon's cell in complete darkness, but to Papillon's delight, there is a chink in the curtain, allowing a sliver of light to come through. It's a brilliant display of hope in time of need. In one scene, McQueen leaps onto the prison bars and whispers, \\"I'm still here, you bastards!\\" Brilliant. Positively thrilling.<br /><br />In many ways, Papillon is a lot like Shawshank Redemption - it's a story of a man who never stopped trying to escape from prison, a man who had indomitable will, great personal strength, and great patience. It's a memorable classic with great actors and acting, sets, characters, and memorable scenes.<br /><br />Strong Recommendation!s are silent, without dialogue. B00ARA4SLU|2.0|I'm a huge fan of Alfred Hitchcock.  I think most people are, or even if they aren't they can certainly appreciate the impact he's had on film in general.  Fewer names are as known as Hitchcock's, and fewer films are as associated with that name as `Psycho'.  So, when you have a film devoted to exposing what went on behind the scenes of the filming of that monumental film, you have my attention.<br /><br />Sadly, this doesn't all work out like I had expected.<br /><br />The film shuffles a few angles to try and compose a well-rounded look at Hitch and his process, but it doesn't all come together like it thinks it does.  Opening with a shot of Ed Gein killing his brother and then having Hitchcock infamously narrate the proceedings, `Hitchcock' opens with promise.  Moving from there to the aftermath of success from Hitchcock's `North by Northwest' and the struggle to find something new and exciting, we see him set his sights on `Psycho', a novel inspired by Gein's killings and something far too violent and grisly for Paramount to accept.  So, Hitchcock decides to fund it himself, and he goes to work.  We see his relationship with his wife, Alma, and her need to more recognition and purpose and the fact that her friend Whitfield Cook continually tries to woo her.  We see his onset antics, his paternal relationship with Leigh and his vile contempt for Vera Miles.  We see him fight with his inner demons as well as the studio, and yet it all feels so thankless.<br /><br />That is really the only word that comes to mind when I think of this film.  It's thankless.  Nothing really stands out as noteworthy, other than those sets and costumes.  The performances are uniformly decent, but no one really stands out other than maybe Mirren, who shades Alma in a way that feels lived in.  Johansson, Biel, Collette and D'Arcy are barely there at all, Huston is certainly a stereotype with little meat to hold him together and Hopkins looks too much like DeVito's Penguin to make much of a real impact.  The interwoven storylines, particularly those involving Gein, serve as an interesting concept and yet don't really add much to the overall film itself.  I felt like Hitch was never really explored.  All of the horror stories revolving around his domineering, egotistical and sexist ways was never presented as anything more than a fleeting possibility.  It is far too sentimental a film to have much bite.<br /><br />I loved the idea of it being a comedic take on the man, full of cheeky fun and insight and yet it just felt bland and uninteresting.a real impact. B0010SB6OK|4.0|With this book, I discovered another interesting British science fiction author, Alastair Reynolds. And what a find! He, like Hamilton, embodies my perfect author of such books as his novels fall under the space opera but rich of almost plausible technology, talk about the future of humanity, are pretty long and complex, but also really imaginative. Oh yes, because Reynolds has really a fantasy out of the ordinary. Not everyone can conceive of a story like that of \\"Century Rain\\".<br />I'll try to define the main points of the plot without spoiling it.<br />\\"Century Rain\\" is set in a future where Earth has been destroyed by nanotechnology. On scary nanotechnology I had recently read \\"Prey\\" by Crichton, however, the main theme here is something else. I do not like at all post-apocalyptic stories, but the so-called nanocaust spoken of in this book is just a detail of the plot and defines the environment in which the story moves.<br />Human survivors live in space stations orbiting the planet. Among them is the main female character, Verity Auger, an archaeologist expert in Paris, which is now just a ghost town. Auger is involved in a very special mission. On Phobos (one of the satellites of Mars) a wormhole was discovered that connects two distant parts of the galaxy. At the other end they found a huge sphere, inside which is a \\"functioning\\" replica of Earth, as it was in 1959. An alien species (undefined) has created many replicas of our planet, including this one that you can access. But the timeline in which these humans live in ignorance is a bit different from that of the true twentieth century.<br />These are the premises. The story is located somewhere between space opera, hard sci-fi, thriller, espionage and time travel, although you do not really travel in time. The way in which it is built is really intriguing, with well-defined characters. The book is very long, because so many things happen, which are difficult to predict, and this makes it very entertaining.<br />Yet even in this case, I stopped at four stars. The reason is simple: in the end the author, in my opinion, did not play his cards right. Being British, I would have expected something outside the box and instead Reynolds seems to have lost himself in the thick of it. Apart from the fact that the love story between the protagonists develops too abruptly and is not at all credible, perhaps because of that a bit too cold, but above all unnatural, look given to the female protagonist by the author (as it often happens when a male author moves a female protagonist), and then that story ends just as suddenly. Even if its end could be explained by a too fast start, two inconsistencies put together, however, do not generate a realistic event, but instead make things worse. For if you forgive the first one, you cannot do the same for the second one.<br />But the worst is right at the end. In this regard, suffice it to say that the characters, after all they've been through, find themselves exactly to the starting point. She seems to have learned nothing. He grew up, instead, but in fact he finds himself again in the condition in which he \\"lived\\" at the beginning of the story. Despite the beautiful prose and the poetic image of the last scene, I was disappointed. An author of this kind, capable of conceiving a story like that, should be more daring.<br />As a justification for the author I must, however, say that the ending is left quite open, allowing readers to imagine how it could continue, perhaps with a better ending.<br />Despite all this, then, I highly recommend reading this book to science fiction lovers who at the same time do not disdain some vintage vibe.<br /><br />Rita Carla Francesca Monticelli, author of [[ASIN:B00L979374 Red Desert - Point of No Return]]g.<br />Yet even in this case, I stopped at four stars. B002G1YPH0|3.0|I mounted this cooler on an Intel I-7 processor in an ASUS P8Z77 motherboard.  The mb would not post and the memory LED light stayed on indicating a problem with memory.  After contacting ASUS, I followed their directions about checking for bent cpu pins (no pins on this processor) and erasing the cmos.  The problem persisted.<br /><br />Then I noticed that the mb would post if the pc case were laying on its side but would not post if the pc case was in the upright (default) position.  This led me to discover that if I put a slight amount of pressure on the cooler (pushing it towards the cpu)while the pc case is in the upright position, the mb would post.  Apparently while the pc case is in the upright position and the cooler is then perpendicular to the pc case and motherboard, the heavy cooler causes the cpu to dislodge just enough to cause the aforementioned symptoms.<br /><br />I have tightened all the screws on the cooler mounting assembly but I suspect that any jarring or movement of the pc case may cause the problem again.  I may go back to the Intel supplied cooler, just to be safe.

Certainly THE IMPOSSIBLE  achieves that. On December 26, 2004 one of the most disastrous tsunamis in history devastated portions of Thailand, killing thousands of people and destroying the landscape and lives of many more. This is the story of a Spanish family living in Japan but vacationing in Thailand and how they coped and survived this impossible natural act. 0385349947|5.0|Sheryl Sandberg offers a powerful, fresh voice on the roles and challenges of both women and men professionally and personally in the world today. She says \\"Lean In: Women, Work, and the Will to Lead\\" is not a memoir, although she has included stories about her life, it's not a self-help book, although she truly hopes it helps and it's not a book on career management, although she offers advice. It's only partly a feminist manifesto as she hopes it inspires both women and men who want to understand what a woman is up against so he can do his part to build an equal world.<br />She combines personal anecdotes with hard data and compelling research to cut through the layers of ambiguity and bias surrounding the lives and choices of working women. She says thirty years after<br />women became 50 percent of the college graduates in the United States, men still hold the vast majority of leadership positions in government and industry.<br />The 2003 Heidi/Howard study Sheryl cited was especially helpful. Two professors from universities in New York ran an experiment to test perceptions of men and women in the workplace. Half the students were assigned to read a story about a successful male venture capitalist called Howard. The other half read the same story but under the name of a female called Heidi. Although their accomplishments were identical the students said Howard came across as appealing and Heidi was seen as selfish and not \\"the type of person you would want to hire or work for.\\" Decades of subsequent research confirm the Heidi/Howard study. They show how success and likability are positively correlated for men and negatively correlated for women and how people are evaluated based on the stereotypes of gender, race, nationality and age. Sheryl believes this bias is at the very core of why women hold themselves back.<br />When a woman focuses on results rather than pleasing others, she's acting like a man. To protect themselves from being disliked, many women question their abilities, downplay their achievements and put themselves down before others can. Author Ken Auletta observed in his \\"New Yorker\\" article that for women \\"self-doubt\\" becomes a form of self defense.<br />Ken Chenault (CEO of American Express) told Sheryl he observed both men and women interrupting women at meetings and giving guys credit for an idea first proposed by a women. He said he now stops the meeting and points out the interruption. It makes participants think twice. Sheryl said she witnessed a similar incident at a business executive dinner. The guest of honor listened to and answered the men's questions politely but barked, \\"Let me finish!\\" and chastised the women for interrupting when they asked questions. A male CEO pulled Sheryl aside afterwards and said he noticed how the women were silenced.<br />Sheryl makes a compelling case for women to lean in, for being ambitious in any pursuit and to \\"Make their partner a real partner.\\" She says, \\"Career progression often depends upon taking risks and advocating for oneself - traits that girls are discouraged from exhibiting.\\" Women hold back in ways both big and small. They are hindered by the external barriers of society and the internal barriers that exist within themselves. They continue to internalize the cultural messages that say it's wrong for a women to be outspoken, aggressive, and more powerful than men. Lacking self-confidence and pulling back they lower their own expectations, do the majority of the housework and childcare, compromise career goals, and compared to their male colleagues, fewer aspire to senior positions. She says women's progress in achieving leadership roles has stalled, their voices are still not heard equally especially in the decisions that most affect their lives.<br />Sheryl is aware there is no one definition of success or happiness. \\"We each have to chart our own<br />course and define what goal fits our lives, values and dreams.\\" Her goal is to help empower both women and men make the shift, person by person, to a more equal world.<br />Sheryl is the chief operating officer of Facebook and is ranked on Fortune's list of the 50 Most Powerful Women in Business and as one of Time's 100 Most Influential People in the World. In 2010, she gave an amazing TEDTalk where she described how women unintentionally hold themselves back in their careers. Her talk that encouraged women to \\"sit at the table,\\" seek challenges, speak their truth, take risks and pursue goals with gusto has been viewed more than two million times.<br />Sheryl's story is my story and I suspect the story of every woman.men and men make the shift, person by person, to a more equal world.<br />Sheryl is the chief operating officer of Facebook and is ranked on Fortune's list of the 50 Most Powerful Women in Business and as one of Time's 100 Most Influential People in the World. B000001GH9|5.0|Instead of a formal review, I'll give you a couple of anecdotes to ponder.<br />In the rehearsal disc that accompanied the original LP box of Solti's recording of Tristan and Isolde, Birgit Nilsson caught herself having held high a note a little longer than prescribed in the score. Solti refused to re-make the scene, laughing and commenting: \\"What's half a second? Wagner never heard it as good!\\" When Yehudi Menuhin visited Bartok in New York in preparation of his performance of a Bartok sonata, Bartok was waiting for him, pencil in hand and without a word of greeting waited for Menuhin to get ready. They played the first movement, and at last Bartok stopped to look at the violinist and said, after a short pause: \\"I thought they played composer's music like this only after they're dead!'<br />A comment like this might have been appropriate in Chicago on the day of this recording.<br />Berg should have lived that long, so as to hear Mutter playing his concerto!

B000007OTH|5.0|When we examine reasons and causes for the neglect of a composer who, in his own lifetime, enjoyed a great reputation, we tend often to forget the changes in society - trends, fashions and the rise to fame of rivals. Bruch acquired fame much earlier than Brahms (who suffered many years from the over-the-top encomium of Schumann). His Concerto in G minor became an instant \\"classic\\", so much so that he was invited by Sarasate to write another for him. But he decided to throw in his lot with opera and oratorio - to us a strange misconception of his own talents. He wanted to match Weber and outdo Wagner by writing a truly \\"national\\" German opera, which never came off as he hoped; and he produced several oratorios and \\"Liedertafel\\" opuses that fell by the wayside when the fashion for this kind of entertainment subsided. Apart from which they also suffered the debility of a brand of late romantic sentimentality that was already on the way out.<br />That's one fairly obvious cause for the dive in his reputation. Another, practically disregarded today, is the fact that music was mostly played at home in that era, which meant that you had to buy expensive scores and study them, before you could play them. Apart from the usual problems of serious composers having to contend in an open market with overtly popular music, there was the issue for aficiodos that so much money and effort would naturally focus on \\"the best\\". In this regard, Bruch was overtaken by Brahms and Dvorak in instrumental music, and eventually (as mentioned) also fell by the wayside in vocal music.<br />As a symphonist, he did not achieve the same high level of excellence as the two  competitors I just named. I think we should disregard Bruckner in this connection, who was hardly well known prior to his 7th Symphony. (We plaster his present fame on him retrospectively and anachronistically. In life, Bruckner influenced no-one.) Bruch steadfastly maintained the tradition of Schumann and Mendelssohn, going beyond them only in terms of (slightly) advanced harmonic diction and some dilution of the formal symphonic genre. This can bring on a sense of deja-vu on first hearing, it's all so easy on the ear and this can deceive us that there areno depths to be discovered. Yet the music is brilliantly crafted, extremely melodious, beautifully orchestrated and rhythmically surprisingly bold. If we ignore the timeline and place him side by side with his forebears, we might on closer acquaintance come to the view that at least two of his symphonies are superior specimens of that art than what Schumann bequeathed on us! However, I will leave that as a mere suggestion, because it is obviously an opinion that can be argued for or against until the cows come home.<br />His 1st Symphony is clearly an apprenticeship exercise, with loads of padding whenever Bruch lost the thread of the symphonic argument. Despite this weakness it is a pretty good Symphony No. 1 when compared to many others! The strongest of the three is No. 2, a very bold and dramatic production with a stark, almost tragic feel to it. This is the work where he comes closest to seriously challenging Brahms - it is (to my mind) a work of equal merit to Brahms' First; but Brahms of course went on to compose No. 4, which left Bruch far behind.<br />No. 3 is the most approachable of the trio. Based on sketches that precede even No. 1, it has a young and vibrant texture and a slow movement of almost voluptuous erotic intensity. It was written, by the way, for the New York Philharmonic, although Bruch could not accede to the invitation to conduct the world premiere owing to prior obligations.<br />These works have not been recorded often enough. So many of Schumann and Mendelssohn, not to mention Saint-Saens, Franck, Bizet and lesser lights; so few of Bruch! My first ever Bruch symphony was an RCA LP of the Louisville Orchestra and Jorge Mester, which made no impact whatever, nor did it anything for me. Marco Polo had one or theother poverty-stricken recording available (some reappearing on the Naxos label). None of this was conducive to improving Bruch's repute as a symphonist. The situation has improved somewhat of late, but hardly enough!<br />So we today have the choice of Masur, Conlon and a handful of single discs.<br />I will begin with Masur, who is far and away the best craftsman and the most sympathetic interpreter of these symphonies; moreover he has far and away the best orchestra at his command of all rivalling issues. I will merely draw attention to the majesty and inexorable propulsion of his No. 2, and the sheer exuberance of his rhythmic textures in No. 3, which also features a red-hot sensual climax for the slow movement of this symphony. The sound quality is not the best, judging by our standards; but it is clear, warm and natural, hardly subject to complaints.<br />Conlon cannot aspire to rivalry here. He is too cool and uninvolved and somehow seems to miss the essence of Bruch's style altogether. His rhythms sound fussy and laboured, and the orchestral execution is also somewhat below par. This set is not competitive when measured against the Masur recordings. Although at first hearing the sound itself seems better than Masur's, on extended listening an artifical hardness manifests itself that becomes the more unpleasant as it goes on.<br />Far more serious work comes from Hickox (London Symphony) on Chandos, although the conductor died before he could complete the cycle. In both No. 1 and 3, he provides a serious challenge to Masur. But he does not displace his rival - not only because No. 2 is missing, but also because Hickox's style is more subdued, less passionate, indeed in greater proximity to Brahms than to Bruch! His rhythms are not as well sprung as we get from Masur; and the \\"love interest\\" in No. 3 seems to leave him cold. Maybe he didn't realise this was love music? As it is he did not get around to No. 2, the touchstone of Bruch stature as a symphonist, where the Brahmsian flavour might have spoken in his favour. Of the two symphonies he published, I can only say that they are fine reading, but lack ultimate conviction. He does, however, enjoy the gamorous sound of Chandos and the company of Mordkovitch, who plays two of the violin concertos as fill-ups to each disc. However, I can't consider these other than to note in passing that Masur's Accardo is by far the better fiddler of the two.<br />So where we stand, it has to be acknowledged that Masur's recording of the symphonies remains first choice. Indeed I am inclined to the view that this set belong among his best recordings.

B0000069CT|4.0|As a complete set this needs to fear no rivalry. It is a classic in its own right; and as far as I know, there is no other complete issue anyway. However, it does not maintain the same high level of excellence throughout, and arguably the collector might do better buying single issues -- for two reasons.<br />Bruch's concertos after the Scottish Fantasy exhibit a marked downturn in the level of inspiration. His ideas convey the impression that they are the fruit of hard labour and they lacks the easy spontaneity of the other works in the genre. So you might not be inclined to submit yourself to them more than once, from curiosity perhaps. Certainly I feel that hearing them for the third or fourth time, boredom takes over at the predictability of the course of these works, and the impression of forcing inspiration gets stronger.<br />I don't want to discourage anyone who likes complete sets. I've already agreed it is a classic. But lovers of Bruch's better known concertos will find in No. 2 a far more sensitive interpreter with Perlman on EMI, who also includes the Scottish Fantasy in a wonderful performance. The latter work is also available in superb readings by Oistrakh and Heifetz. For No. 1 the choice is wide. But to stay in Leipzig, there is Vengerov with Masur in an indubitably more elegant and refined reading that Accordo gives here. More recommendations are probably unnecessary. But my 4 stars simply reflect the state of the market, where those items which would be of primary interest to a buyer are placed among some of the hottest competition of any music!

The other was more typical, [[ASIN:0143121243 The Idea of America: Reflections on the Birth of the United States]]  presented interesting topics, but a good essay or two was packed between a lot of filler.  In other collections, I cringe as essays ramble or serve as vanity pieces.<br /><br />Like a Marukami novel this book of essays is quirky and satisfying.  I liked the unifying theme. Strangely I thought the last essay was the weakest.<br /><br />Marukami tells about how marathon running is done. He explains why, but to a non-runner like myself, the cost-benefit ratio does not seem balanced. It's endless. Distance logs. Shoes.  Pain. Even massages hurt. Knees give out. Legs give out when you don't expect it. After a double marathon race, he couldn't walk for two days. There is a discussion of aging and diminishing performance.<br /><br />There are many observations on life and his work. I learned a lot about him. He once owned a jazz bar. Now  he obsessively collects jazz and pop albums and CDs, hence the many references to music in his work. He's run 26 marathons and participated in 4 triathlons. He's translated quite a number of classics from English to Japanese.<br /><br />Buried in here is an autobiography. For Murakami his running, like his writing, is part of who he is.<br /><br />This is an unusual look at a gift writer who has spanned cultures with his literature..

You can always look it up online if you are one of the few that are unfamiliar with the game Hangman.<br /><br />Instead I want to focus on what I think about this version of Hangman. I have concluded that this app is meant to be an Educational/Game type app by the app developer Ensenasoft, and that is exactly what the problem with it is.  It is OK at both but does not do a really good job at being either type of app.<br /><br />Like other previous free app of the day titles that Ensenasoft has offered, there are different \\"skins\\" including a holiday one.  Again another sign that this is a product trying to be many different things.  They included a feature after the word is revealed that allows you to go to one of the dictionary websites to look up the definition of the word you just tried to guess.  This is one sign of the attempt to make this app educational.  There are also words used in this Hangman game that are unfamiliar to most such as colugo or a narwahl under the animal category.  Imagine an episode of Wheel of Fortune where the puzzle is colugo or narwahl. Using words like colugo or narwahl is good for educational value, but not for game value since very few people would actually be able to guess it.<br /><br />But they also try to make this a game by putting in some nice animations of the stickman as he is being drawn. B00CDTSQZW|3.0|Many, many. many bad viking movies have been made... More than fifty years ago Kirk Douglas and his production company produced what most people still consider the &#34;best&#34; Viking movie ever made, The Vikings.<br /><br />Given what information available at the time, they made a good faith effort to do the thing relatively authentically. And they made a really bad movie. We have learned so much in the fifty years since, especially due to advances in archaeology, and many scholars and reenactors around the world (of whom I am among the least) work hard to authentically depict Viking Age life.<br /><br />Many very accurate replicas of Viking ships have been built, to the wonder of many, have actually sailed these vessels to the distant lands that the vikings explored. Any moderately accurate depiction of the Viking Age would far surpass Kirk Douglas's film in portraying of one of the most exciting and colorful eras in human history.<br /><br />The History Channel's new series, Vikings, is simply not very historically accurate.<br /><br />I could say a lot about the many technical faults of this series -- the ridiculous idea that 8th or 9th Century Scandinavians had no knowledge of the British Isles, the placement of the &#34;steering board&#34; on the wrong side of the ship, the really, really &#34;creative&#34; costuming -- but my biggest disappointment on the basis of viewing the first eight episodes is the blatantly wrong philosophical and political underpinnings of the series. There is clearly a political ideology being flogged here, and it's more significant to our current history than you probably think.<br /><br />The primary villain of the series is the chieftain who rules over our hero Ragnar Lodbrok (Travis Fimmel), a man called Jarl (Earl) Haraldson (Gabriel Byrne). This title formulation, by the way, is a silly one, basically equivalent to calling the modern queen of England &#34;Queen Windsor.&#34; Jarl Haraldson rules like a medieval feudal lord, dominating the governing assembly (the &#34;Thing&#34;), bullying and threatening everyone. Our hero, Ragnar, dreams of sailing West to raid the British Isles, but the Jarl insists there is no such place. He threatens Ragnar with the confiscation of his property if he does not obey, and raid East instead.<br /><br />This is a horribly inaccurate depiction of the political system of the Vikings. Lazy, contemporary, and &#34;progressive&#34; minded scriptwriters are prone to writing stories about some dynamic young person (who wants freedom) in conflict with a hidebound old conservative, who lives by stifling oppression.<br /><br />My major problem with a series coming from a network that calls itself the &#34;History Channel&#34; -- is that this is precisely the opposite of the political dynamic that was actually playing out during the Viking Age. In the Viking Age, there was indeed an old tradition -- but it was a tradition of freedom and democracy. And there were plenty of fresh new ideas about -- but they were ideas of the new elite: centralization and autocracy.<br /><br />Prof. Torgrim Titlestad of the University of Stavanger has written a thesis on Viking society (which had roots in earlier Germanic tribalism of the sort described by Tacitus) was essentially democratic, centered in local, district, and regional Things, with authority vested in twelve-man courts and &#34;Lawspeakers,&#34; whose duty it was to recite one-third of the law each year. These were not egalitarian assemblies -- the local chieftains certainly did have power and they exercised it -- but the vikings were essentially democratic, and every free man had a voice at a Thing.<br /><br />As depicted in the Vikings series, the Thing is held in the Jarl's longhouse and is totally under his, and his creature's, control. The Jarl acts in ways the real Vikings of that time would never have tolerated. One of the old Norwegian Thing laws makes it a civil duty to rebel against a chieftain who is overbearing and selfish. Things were also held in the open air, in a central, neutral location offering no special advantage to the chieftain or any other free man.<br /><br />Now, it is accurate that the Viking Age was an age of change in Scandinavia. The old democratic Thing system came under attack from &#34;progressives&#34; who believed they had a better way to govern. These new thinkers were primarily Christians who had learned these radical new ideas in continental Europe. These new ideas argued that everything would work much better if all the power was placed in the hands of a king, who would centrally manage the realm, Feudalism. The old democratic Things, in this view, were outmoded relics of a barbaric and heathen past, they needed to be stamped out and replaced by a state religion and a more centralized government.<br /><br />The good folks at the History Channel got it so totally wrong. Telling this truth about the vikings would be akin to a 21st Century Hollywood blasphemy.<br /><br />That all being said, the series is still pretty entertaining. They got a whole lot of things right. The production values are very high. The characters are interesting, well developed, and they are well acted. The sets, locations, and lighting are artistic and beautiful to look at. Is the series perfect? No, there are many mistakes, that real scholars of the period will no doubt notice, but rewriting history to pervert an entire society's system of government because of a progressive modern political bent is just dead wrong.ng and selfish. Camille Preaker is a reporter for a Chicago newspaper who has been assigned to cover the murder of two girls in her hometown of Wind Gap, Missouri.  Once she gets home, old feelings come back to haunt her from her childhood.  This is compounded by the fact that her younger sister was murdered in the same manner as the two girls.<br /><br />While this would sound like a run-of-the-mill murder mystery, this story is really about Camille, her sister Amma and their mother Amora.  The mother never seemed to recover from the grief of losing a daughter and both of the girls have suffered in other ways.  Some of Camille's recollections are downright disturbing, such as losing her virginity at 13 to not one but four boys (\\"I got more action that night than I have the last ten years\\"), and her cutting.  The cutting she did spelled out words across her entire body, save for a \\"crop circle\\" on her back.  Amma, currently 13, is also acting out in manners that are signs of troubled teenagers.<br /><br />The murder investigation, and stories for Camille's paper, are still progressing through the story and the former takes many twists and turns, while the latter becomes an afterthought as Camille goes through many emotions as memories and interactions with her mother become entangled.  One interesting aspect of the murder investigation is that the lead detective Richard and Camille sleep together.  Not necessarily for the sex, but because one person has information the other wants for their work.<br /><br />The reader really gets into why Camille is so tortured and while painful to read at times, are well-written and draw the reader into her soul.  A great read that I gave 4  stars.  My only reason for slightly less than 5 was the overly disturbing aspects of Camille's and Amma's life that shook me.<br /><br />Did I skim? No<br /><br />Did I feel connected to the characters?  No, but that is good.  These characters' flaws, especially those in Camille and Amma, were portrayed with such vivid detail that not many readers could really \\"relate\\" even if they shared some of those characteristic in their personal lives.<br /><br />Pace of the story:  I felt it was a bit slow. The secondary characters such as Richard, the Kansas City detective, and John, the prime suspect, are not forgotten as the reader understands what they are going through as well.<br /><br />Negatives:  While the character development is the selling point of the book, I felt at times it undermined the story of the murder investigation. 0307352153|4.0|\\"Quiet: The Power of Introverts in a World That Can't Stop Talking\\" is a book that has been sitting in my to-do list for a few months now. I'd heard about the hype surrounding the book and its premise - to challenge the misconceptions of what it means to be an introvert. As an introvert myself, I decided it was time to discover if this was another self-inflated attempt at psychology-made-easy, or if \\"Quiet\\" really was a new window into the world of the introverted self.<br /><br />At first glance, the prose is unassuming and easy to read. The author touches on the reality of today: our world is designed for the extrovert - from the group desks and mandatory group work in our schools to the open plan offices in our workplaces and the focus on presentation skills and forceful conversation. The author illuminates the bitter reality - that introversion, by and large, has been considered an unwanted trait: a concern of the parents of shy children, a personal development issue for the manager of an employee who enjoys working alone, a constant worry of an individual who would rather read a book than attend a dinner party.<br /><br />Through careful exposition, the author sources a number of thought-provoking studies that reveal the hidden power of introverts. These include: creativity (70% of all creative are introverts), persistence, concentration, insight, sensitivity and the ability to solve complex problems. There are many areas in which introverts excel in comparison to their extroverted brethren and these are only a few of the numerous insights within \\"Quiet\\".<br /><br />The author's research is excellent. She sources from a number of studies and findings, conveying the material in a manner that is both revealing and easy to digest. Reinforcing the work are the various stories from interviewees, introverts themselves who have grown up in a world (mostly) designed for extroverts. The stories break the monotony of the text, making the reading of \\"Quiet\\" an enjoyable experience rather than a taxing one. I was truly surprised by the depth of the author's research - indeed, the final 30% of the book is dedicated to the reference table and many scientific studies which the author makes use of.<br /><br />\\"Quiet\\" is a satisfying and meaningful presentation of the benefits of the introverted self. If you, a partner or a member of your family is an introvert, I would highly recommend this book as a means of exploring the introverted self and the power that lies within. If you are a manager, this book should already be in your bookshelf as a critical guide to understanding (and appreciating) the various dimensions of your employees. Lastly, parents of introverted children should welcome the book's findings with open arms as a new means of connecting with your child.<br /><br />Overall, this is an expertly written book; well-researched and simple enough to digest. The lessons that the author seeks to teach us about the introverted self are clear - awareness, understanding and appreciation.<br /><br />Go on. Try the book. I dare say it may transform your life.xperience rather than a taxing one. All of Bob Marley's previous deluxe editions have come with this slipcase. I know you are thinking, &#34;how educational is Zombie Dice!!!??&#34; BUT!! What I do is use the dice as a reward for answering a question. Each student (usually a group of 4 or less) gets asked a question (it depends on the subject what the question might be.) If they get it right, they get to roll. I usually give them hints or something so they don't lose their turn entirely. The first person to reach 13 BRAIIIIINS! is the winner.<br /><br />FOR YOUNGER KIDS (or just to make it eaiser): I made my own little score sheet (I posted an image above in the product photos):<br /><br />It is simply a paper that has the rules and 13 squares. On their turn, the student gets the dice (after their question of course!) They roll. If they have 3 &#34;shotguns&#34; or more...their turn is over. If they have less than that they get to choose to keep rolling or count the brains they have rolled so far. They separate out the shotguns so they can keep track of how close to &#34;3&#34; they are. When they wish to stop (before reaching the limit of 3 shotguns) they cross out that many brains on their scoresheet. Play continues until one player passes the 13 mark. We don't really get into the red, yellow and green colors with most younger students. With older kids, we talk about the sides of the red dice versus the other colors and how the probability is more difficult when you are rolling mostly red dice (versus rolling mostly greens.) Edit: I should have noted that I changed the word &#34;shotgun&#34; to &#34;strike&#34; when I use this at school. &#34;Three strikes you're out&#34; is actually easier for them to remember anyway.<br /><br />It is really a motivating game...I am not even sure why. I almost thought that it might be scary or something for kids, but it is not. They ALL request to play it. B008JFUO4U|4.0|OBLIVION is a gorgeous movie to look at.  It's a marvel of brown and gray.  Tom Cruise is a charismatic movie star.  Those two elements alone make the film worth seeing.  And it's a good thing OBLIVION has those things going for it, because much of the rest is so-so at best.<br /><br />I appreciated the steady , slow-paced build of this film...it is not very action heavy, short of one extended (and disjointed) shootout in an underground bunker facility.  We really mostly spend A LOT of time in closeup with Tom Cruise, with a gorgeously ravaged earth all around him.<br /><br />We meet Cruise as he's setting out on another \\"drone repair\\" mission to earth...and earth that was essentially obliterated during a war with alien invaders.  We won the war, but had to abandon the ruined planet.  Cruise and his partner/lover (Andrea Riseborough) are all alone on a station build well above the surface of the planet.  Below, the cities and farmlands are all covered in dunes.  The top of the Empire State Building is all that pokes through the surface...most of the rest of NYC is buried.  Offshore, gigantic machines, serviced by drones, are pulling up the water from our oceans and are used up in a fusion process to bring energy to Saturn's moon, Titan...where the remnants of humanity have fled.<br /><br />Riseborough (an actress I don't know well, but who makes for an interesting, striking figure) stays on the station, while Cruise yearns to spend more and more time on earth.  He has no memories of the planet, but he has an infinite interest in what life was like there.  His passion for his work and for the planet are what draw us to his character.  Cruise is seldom a truly great actor.  But he's one of those stars (like Clooney) who are charismatic and engaging.  Cruise is usually helped by having a character with a dark side or a past...and we get hints of both.  He carries himself with both great physical assurance AND air of sadness.<br /><br />And the earth around him, bleak and nearly empty, is a CGI wonder.  I know that almost all of it is completely \\"fake\\" but the special effects teams have done a stellar job in creating a convincing, post-apocalyptic world that is lovely to look at.  I enjoyed the many scenes where Cruise flies around.  I'm so tired of bloated CGI worlds, be they Middle Earth or Thor's home planet or any other gilded, over-stuffed fantasy world.  OBLIVION feels \\"tasteful.\\"  The color palette, from Riseborough's clothing to the hue of the sand dunes, is soothing and creative.<br /><br />Needless to say, the movie is not just about Cruise flying around earth.  Slowly and surely, the reality he thought he knew begins to come under assault, both from external factors and from bits of memory that come back to him.  I won't say more, because the movie wants you to slowly unravel its mysteries.  I won't spoil them, although I think they are not terribly inventive in the long run.  Eventually, other characters are introduced, and they are not well fleshed out, even for a sci-fi story.  Olga Kurylenko appears and I left to wonder again (as I did for QUANTUM OF SOLACE and HIT MAN) how she gets large movie roles.  She is bland and uninteresting.  Morgan Freeman is on hand, but does little more than talk like Freeman...it's barely a performance.<br /><br />I'll say again, it's a good thing the experienced and engaging Cruise is on hand to be our tour guide.  With a less compelling star, the less-original-than-its-creators-think plot and the mostly non-existent character development would doom this film.  As it is, I found enough here to like and to recommend the film.  I give it 3.5 stars, but will round up to 4.

B003VR5PPY|5.0|I just unlocked my fourth of the six endings, and I'm trying to convince myself to do chores instead of continuing to play. I think my efforts to convince myself of this will be in vain.<br /><br />I have never been one for story lines in games, but this one is not only necessary but incredibly interesting. The characters have intertwined relationships that slowly reveal themselves through game play.<br /><br />As other reviewers have mentioned, with each play-through, you do have to go through every conversation again. Even with fast-forwarding, I find this at least a little bit annoying. However, it's completely worth it. I keep thinking that each play-through will reveal very little more than the previous, but I have always been wrong so far. I've only played 5 times so far, but I would still call that excellent replay value (which was obviously the intention).<br /><br />It's pretty obvious that they put way more into the budget of the story and general programming than images/animation, but the former are so good that it does not matter even the slightest that there are just a few sprites per character.<br /><br />My advice is to take very detailed notes with each run-through. Note whom you take with you, which path you take, how you respond to people's questions, etc. Most definitely note which doors you go through and solutions to puzzles as well. Draw out maps of each level, and write where you find each key and key card as well as where you have to go to use each.<br /><br />Every run has been an absolute blast. The music gets your heart racing, and comments the other characters make get the gears turning about the story's background. They're often funny in their wit as well.<br /><br />I most definitely agree with others that this game is in no way for children, which honestly makes it a great go-to game for adults like me who love the DS (because so much is geared to a younger audience with the DS). If you are easily offended by curse words, gory descriptions, and the occasional highly suggestive comment, this game is certainly not for you. For the rest, you need to make this the next game on your list and prepare to be hooked.

0385349947|4.0|Sheryl Sandberg is earnest.  She really thinks she's figured out some solutions for helping women rise to the top.  Women need to stop holding themselves back, speak up and really be more assertive, women need to help each other, women need to stop feeling guilty, workplaces need to be more accomodating, and, most importantly, they need to find a good man.  I don't disagree with many of these points, but Ms. Sandberg overestimates how easy it is for women to satisfy this formula.  Yes, she's right - a woman needs a good partner.  She even gives tips for finding that perfect man who'll pack lunches and accommodate your ambitious schedule.  Well, no matter how much you plan, things don't always work out so perfectly.  It's hard to predict how Mr. Perfect will behave once the kids are actually there.  Mr. Perfect may not have a flexible schedule (and he may not want one).  Mr. Perfect might face health issues.  Mr. Perfect may turn out to be a cheater.  He may decide marriage is not for him?  And then what?  Ms. Sandberg calls the choice of spouse the single most important career choice a woman can make.  But there's absolutely no way to guarantee a fairytale ending at home or in the workplace.  Are Single mothers out of luck?  She has absolutely no discussion of how single women are supposed to \\"lean in\\" and be constantly available when there's no one to fall back on.<br /><br />As I'm sure other commenters have noted, this book also isn't about \\"regular\\" women.  It's aimed at a very small subset of women - elite women who have the potential to reach the highest echelons in their fields.  I think Ms. Sandberg makes this clear from the beginning, as the question is why aren't there more women CEOs/law firm partners etc.  So I don't think this is a HUGE fault.  However, the subset of women is even tinier as it's aimed at elite women who are either unmarried or are in happy marriages where the husband is a full partner.<br /><br />Ultimately, I think she needs to rethink the idea that a woman's success is dependent on her choice of spouse.  Yes, a great, accommodating, flexible spouse certainly helps. 160774273X|5.0|My sister gave me Ken Forkish's Flour Water Salt Yeast last holiday season. I hadn't heard of the book, but its title let me know plenty by its clear and bold allusion to the French legal restrictions for commercial bread bakeries. I have no culinary background, neither do I have any amateur pretensions in the kitchen. In my profession I taught research writing methods and techniques and technical writing (among other things), and read and evaluated many manuals and textbooks. Also, I lived for a summer outside Paris and travelled in northern France. France's reputation for its breads is justified. Since 1988 I've owned several bread-making machines and have relied on them nearly exclusively, and have occasionally bought breads from artisanal bakeries and other specialty bakeries, such as Great Harvest (for the unique whole-wheat loaves). I've relied on some of the better books for using a bread machine, often only for dough-making while finishing the loaf in the oven; also I've used conventional bread books, interpolating the recipes and methods for my machine.<br /><br />The breads I've made from Ken Forkish's book have been the best breads I've ever had. It wouldn't surprise me to learn that his Flour Water Salt Yeast becomes the standard and authoritative text for most or all culinary institutes and schools; it is outstanding as a text and reference work. More, his personal authority as a master baker appears on every page, his great affection for the most delicious breads always turning the reader's learning into a leavening tutorial, as it were.<br /><br />While reading the book I became convinced that it contained and revealed the most important truths about making bread. Before, I had read deeply about bread making but always more or less despaired from ever making great bread because I've lacked the patience; great bread requires time, many hours of it, days and weeks if it's to be done right with a levain or sourdough. Yet Forkish's thorough explanations and directions gave me new hope. His text is as much about bread philosophy and aesthetics as about technique and method. That is, his book is both scholarly and artful, and rather delightful to read.<br /><br />Being convinced, I purchased a digital scale, oven thermometer, mixing tubs, brotforms, and a very fine 4.5-quart Dutch oven. However, I chose to use my bread machine for the pre-ferment step and for the folding and resting steps. The machine is a Panasonic SD-YD250 I've used for several years; it's a very thoughtful and gentle machine, I've come to know it, and I figured it could produce the right kind of bread dough since it used the same amounts of time for folding and resting as Forkish's book describes. I made half of the book's recipes since the machine cannot accommodate twice the amounts. After the machine completed the final dough formation, I followed Ken's directions scrupulously for shaping, proofing, and baking. I've made all the pre-ferment breads and overnight breads, but have not tried any levains. Every loaf has turned out perfect, most look exactly like the book's pictures, and as I've said, I've never had better bread, anywhere. I believe I've had the same sensory pleasures as Ken describes. Really, it's intoxicating and essentially a new experience and pleasure about bread, like attaining the Holy Grail. My search has ended.<br /><br />I've used King Arthur all-purpose flour, as Ken uses in his home test kitchen, and Trader Joe's all-purpose flour (also King Arthur's whole-wheat and Trader Joe's whole-wheat), unaware of any differences in any of the many qualities of the finished breads.<br /><br />(I've made some pizzas using the book's methods, and I could write pages about the book's insights and my successes, but I'm still experimenting ... .)<br /><br />I halve the finished and cooled loaf, cut half into slices and freeze them. The bread freezes well: I microwave the frozen slice for 10-15 seconds and make toast (which is heavenly).<br /><br />Often, readers look at the first few sentences of a review, notice the review's length, then look at the ending part.<br /><br />I've tried to contribute my experience of combining Ken Forkish's methods in his outstanding Flour Water Salt Yeast book with a modern, full-feature bread-making machine. I'll add that an important feature of a good reference manual is that it lies flat when opened to any page, and this book also does that very well.

The stuff in that book is stuff everyone should know all ready -- how women still aren't treated equally in the work force, how some of that is because we and others have been brainwashed, how some of that is because some people are just jerks, and how, ultimately, in order to change anything, *everyone* has to change, including ourselves.<br /><br />I've seen criticism that Sandberg \\"blames the victim\\". I think the majority of this sentiment comes from people reading articles about the book with information taken out of context. In the book itself, it's really just all about \\"Here are some tactics that can help improve things for everyone, given the current state of affairs. Give them a try.\\" Again, nothing groundbreaking at all.<br /><br />I've seen lots of criticism that Sandberg has lead a privileged life. I'm not sure how that's totally relevant to the book, as all the research and statistics she uses are valid regardless of her upbringing. And, as I keep saying, her advice is not *new*. So her backstory has nothing to do with that advice.<br /><br />Even if you already know all the things Sandberg covers, reading this book helps refresh and reinforce what you may need reminders of. B0000029AL|5.0|Anyone who has read my other reviews knows how much I love debut albums. The first opportunity an artist has to define themselves--to make a statement about who they are and where they come from (their roots). Not usually where they are going, for an artist usually changes and evolves over the course of a career (not always for the better, in my opinion.)For this particular review, I will do things a little different. I will present this album song-by-song, and for those songs I think really stand out I will expound on, and for those I don't really care for--I simply write \\"filler\\" and move to the next. So here it goes.<br /><br />\\"Make It\\"--\\"Good evening people, welcome to the show\\", belts out Steve Tyler as he makes his grand entrance to the rock and roll world. The first words on the first song on the first album by Aerosmith--as memorable an opening line as the first words on the first song on the first album by Led Zeppelin, as Robert Plant spews out \\"In the days of my youth\\" on their classic opener, \\"Good Times Bad Times\\". Musically speaking, it is the most dated sounding song in Aerosmith's repetiore. A lot of people say that their most dated sounding song is \\"Spaced\\" from the second album, \\"Get Your Wings\\". I strongly disagree. Not only was this song, and this album, recorded on much more ancient equipment than \\"Get Your Wings\\", but the song itself features such choppy, mod-punk, Who-Kinks like guitar riffs from Joe Perry and Brad Whitford, forget 1973--this sounds like Aerosmith if they had existed in the 1960s! But it is one of my personal favorites because of it.<br /><br />\\"Somebody\\"--filler (next please)<br /><br />\\"Dream On\\"--What else can be said about this gem that hasn't already. The ultimate heavy rock power ballad, and one that should have been a hit in all of America when it was first released. About the smartest thing Aerosmith ever did was re-release this as a single 3 years later, knowing full well that with their new-found fame, America wouldembrace it..And they did! It should have been saved for last on the album though, instead of buried in the middle<br /><br />\\"One Way Street\\"--Not a style of song that Aerosmith would continue with later, but does contribute to their image of what was later described by the press as \\"New York's Heavy Metal Sound\\". Very urban sounding, with very urban lyrics--reminds me of taking a stroll through the tough streets of Brooklyn or Detroit on a cloudy day, saying \\"hello\\" to the hot dog vendors as I pass by.<br /><br />\\"Mama Kin\\"--Not one of my personal favorites, and a song I think is highly overrated compared to some of the other tracks here. But hey--Guns and Roses dug it!<br /><br />\\"Write Me a Letter\\"--filler (next please)<br /><br />\\"Movin' Out\\"--Amazing what a simple act of being kicked out of your pad would inspire such great lyrics and great guitar riffs! But this is one of my personal favorites from the album, the guitar riffs stick in my head for days!<br /><br />\\"Walkin' The Dog\\"--And speaking of guitar riffs, these are some of the best that Perry and Whitford ever came up with! I never got to hear the original of this song, but their rendition is truly killer and the lyrics fit Steve's style of singing so well, you almost think that they were his own! Also of note--Tyler's wood flute solo in the middle: I never thought you could add a wood flute to this kind of bump-and-grind rhythm and make it sound cool, but Stevie made it work.<br /><br />As a whole, the album sounds vastly different in production, because it was different in production. Whereas the Record Plant, where they would record some of their classic mid-period albums, was state-of-the-art with a state-of-the-art producer, Jack Douglas; this album sounded like it was recorded in a dingy warehouse with a dingy producer, namely Adrian Barber, famous for producing the Allman Brothers debut album with his \\"flip-the-switch-and-let-'em-bang-out-the-tunes-til-we-get-tired-and-fade-em-out\\"-style of production. But both albums bY Aerosmith and the Allmans have a certain charm about them because of that very raw, grimy production, with a style of music and lyrics to match!<br />One more point: Steve Tyler always said he never liked his vocals on this album. In my opinion, he never sounded better AFTER this album. Clean and clear, booming like a trumpet--far removed from the rasp he would develop on the next album. I think he had a very unique voice on this album--reminded me of a rock version of Robert Johnson to match the raw, blues-influenced rock presented here.

This film was unusual in that it really centered on Lincoln's efforts to pass the thirteenth amendment which freed the slaves.  Of course the terrible Civil War was the foremost challenge of his term, but most of this movie is how Lincoln and those close to him went about trying to garner votes for a very controversial amendment.  This is really the first time I've seen Lincoln in a different light.  He comes across as not the eloquent speaker of the Gettysburg Address, but most of the time he is seen as a bumpkin, using bad grammar, telling off color jokes, and really putting across a more human side rather than the larger than life image that we hear about.  Mostly he acted this way around his close advisors and his wife, Mary, and he never appeared shaken and that is probably why the jokes and stories.  It was to deflect from the terrible things that were going on around him.  Sally Field in her role as Mary Lincoln really captures her in looks, mannerisms, and is the long suffering depressed woman that we know.  Tommy Lee Jones as Thaddeus Stephens, the very passionate Congressman who supported Lincoln and the thirteenth amendment did a good job.  I almost didn't recognize him! B00936A0RU|3.0|The opening scenes of \\"Lawless,\\" John Hillcoat's fascinating 2012 work, are so intense they create anticipation the film does not live up to. Tom Hardy plays brother Forrest Bondurant, the formidable protector of a backwoods Virginia moonshine operation during the Prohibition. He has a face-off with a new sheriff of sorts, the preening Charlie Rakes (Guy Pearce) who has arrived by way of Chicago to collect toll. Hardy's stare is so intense it practically cracks the camera lens. After barely a pronounced word and the twitch of a brow, you know instantly his savage code is not to be underestimated. It's a spellbinding moment the film rarely matches.<br /><br />There's a great movie here, a sort of combination of [[ASIN:B00A8OWOSO Miller's Crossing [HD]]] and [[ASIN:B00498YZH8 Winter's Bone]], but somewhere on the muddy back roads it becomes lost in box office ambition. Based on the true story of the Bondurants, with a screenplay adapted by Nick Cave from Matt Bondurant's memoir [[ASIN:B001JQB0GC The Wettest County in the World: A Novel Based on a True Story]], filmmakers cast Shia LaBeouf as the youngest brother -- the runt of the litter -- and primarily focus on his attempts to live the code while courting the preacher's daughter. Such plot developments are as old as James Cagney's socks.<br /><br />LaBeouf is an acquired taste anyway, possessing boyish looks without the slightest menace. Hardy's presence, not to mention Jason Clarke as the fearless older brother Howard, completely swallow LaBeouf's sniveling portrayal. There's a primitive society here (check out the classic documentary [[ASIN:B000E5LEVU Harlan County, U.S.A. (The Criterion Collection)]]), and Hardy and Clarke capture the palpable nature of such blood cults. You need fingernail dirt, and LaBeouf has the presence of a kid folding jeans at The Gap.<br /><br />By way of Australia, Hillcoat has sparked an interesting career with [[ASIN:B002R1QQ9O The Proposition]] and [[ASIN:B003IWRE3I The Road]]. \\"Lawless\\" is a step back, though shows signs of considerable post-production tinkering. One suspects the film clocked at three-plus hours and Hillcoat had to whittle down to two. There's a musical montage that shows the Bondurants rolling in moonshine money while Pearce's Rakes destroys neighboring stills dotting the Virginia mountains. It's the moment the film unravels.<br /><br />\\"Lawless\\" introduces two fascinating characters, including Maggie Beauford, a dame with a past (Jessica Chastain), and gangster Floyd Banner (Gary Oldman), but both are practically forgotten. Chastain especially deserves screen time, working the bar at the Bondurants' rural enterprise and capturing the wary eye of Forrest. There's an unforgettable scene when Chastain, sans clothes, slowly pats down a darkened hallway to his bed, ranking as one of the most haunting snapshots in recent memory. It's the type of image Jean Cocteau would admire ([[ASIN:B00007L4I6 Beauty and The Beast (The Criterion Collection)]]).<br /><br />Such moments in \\"Lawless\\" abound. It's a beautiful film, with the extraordinary cinematography of Benoit Delhomme, on-location filming in rural Georgia and brooding set designs creating a lamentable atmosphere. But the plot flow is too fragmented. You know there's going to be a showdown between Forrest and Sheriff Rakes, and they indeed meet next to a covered bridge surrounded by men toting rusty shotguns. By this point, the film has entirely lost its momentum, and it stumbles to an abrupt ending lacking the poetry Hillcoat was hoping for.<br /><br />Prior to \\"Lawless,\\" I had only seen Hardy in a few blockbuster epics ([[ASIN:B0047WJ12K Inception]]). His performance is a revelation, possessing the presence of mountains. It's a bitter sin we are forced to endure LaBeouf's preening. There's an interesting supporting character, a local handicapped youth named Cricket. Played by relative unknown Dane DeHaan, it's a striking portrait of tragedy. Wouldn't it have been interesting to switch DeHaan and LaBeouf? I'm just not convinced LaBeouf can evolve into the kind of leading man of action \\"Lawless\\" attempts to force-feed us. And when you have Hardy, Clarke, Chastain and Oldman standing in the background with the film's running time slowly ticking away, you know a great opportunity has been lost.

B000V63B90|5.0|OK, let's readily admit that not all of us were fans of 80's pop music. I, for one, suffered through the 80's latching onto the very rare glimpses of creativity that it managed to spawn. (Thank goodness Ska and Punk saved at least some of it) Syncopated drum beats, bad clothes, REALLY bad hair, over production, \\"Logo Bands\\" and throw away dance songs. However, Lexicon of Love is a standout and MAY BE the quintessential 80's pop album. (Not counting \\"Thriller\\" which will always be in a league all its own.) Just as other albums define an era or a type of music, such as Led Zeppelin II defining power rock/blues, Dark Side of the Moon defining 70's progressive music and The Allman Bothers first album defining southern rock; (Personal opinion not fact. So lay off any criticism!) Lexicon defines the excesses and pop music dance beat of the 80's. (I am not saying LOL is as good as these albums either. I am just saying it well represents this era of music.)<br /><br />I must readily admit that I thoroughly enjoy every second of the album. Generally, once I put it on I have to listen to it start to finish. I get hooked and simply can't turn it off. This album is no sleeper. There are no slow ballads. ABC wanted to get you moving and keep you moving throughout the entire album.<br />The song arrangement of the album is darn near perfect. Every song on LOL blends seamlessly into the next. Some are bridged with very short orchestral movements only to break into another drum beat to pull you into the next song. Being a fan of progressive music, this could be why I like this album. It is \\"almost\\" constructed as one continuous piece of music. Each song is catchy with great hooks, a steady beat and fantastic tongue in cheek lyrics. \\"Like the phoenix rising up from the ashes, I know what's good but I know what trash is!\\"  \\"No rhythm in cymbals, no tempo in drums, love on arrival, she comes when she comes.\\" The albums sound is dated; you can tell it is 80's pop within the first 30 seconds so you probably can't put forth the argument that it is \\"timeless\\". But for what it is, pure 80's pop/dance, you are not going to get much better.<br /><br />Martin Fry's voice is along the same lines as Bryan Ferry's with a nice smoky quality, good range, and so much feeling. One might even put forth the argument that ABC was Roxie Music wanna be's. Their music is definitely in the same style although a bit more \\"lush\\" than RM. Where Roxie Music eventually became known for several stellar musicians (I.e. Phil Manzanera, Brian Eno, Andy Mackay, Eddie Jobson to name but a few) I doubt you could say the same for ABC. (However, Roxie Music's original drummer and bass player did end up on ABC's second album \\"Beauty Stab\\".) But, they were good at what they did. They were a good cohesive group. This could be what hurt them. They could never match the success of this LP and just didn't seem to want to stay in this same genre. They tried on their second LP \\"Beauty Stab\\" to be more guitar based and it failed miserably. Which really puzzles me because it is a more creative album and showed off a very talented band. It was as equally energetic as LOL but did move away from dance, which may have allienated their fan base a bit too soon. \\"Beauty Stab\\" is a solid 4 star album with a much stronger new wave and rock beat. While not quite as good, I think it may hold up a bit better then LOL by not sounding quite as dated some 30 years later.<br /><br />It will be difficult to find a bad review of this album. From its onset it was well received. It went straight to the top of the UK charts and still shows up on the list of the top 100 British albums. Not bad for what many consider throw away 80's dance music.
